{"status":200,"result":{"posts":[{"platformId":"1470762849886760_1785515361744839","platform":"Facebook","date":"2017-09-05 14:58:36","updated":"2021-09-07 16:31:33","type":"photo","message":"“It was 1971 when I finished my medical college in Mangalore. It was the time of the Indo-Pak war which culminated in the formation of Bangladesh. My Dean asked me if I would be willing to volunteer as a “senior physician” to take care of refugees crossing into India. I immediately agreed. Being the only child of my mother, she hesitated for just one minute before she said that I should do what God has brought us to do in this world. It was an eye-opening experience. When you see people leaving behind everything that they own to save their lives and be happy with what little is provided, it humbles you. Soon after marrying my college sweetheart, we moved to Uganda, E. Africa. The training and practice of medicine in Africa taught me so much about caring for the poor of the poorest. After Idi Amin expelled all non- Ugandans, we emigrated to the USA. Despite the fact I had several offers from various clinics and individual practitioners to join them, we chose to open my medical practice in Winslow, a small town in northeastern Arizona with a population of 9000. I had an open-door policy of never refusing any patient regardless of their insurance or ability to pay for medical care. On one occasion, a family of four from San Diego (husband, wife and two small children) walked into my office. The two small girls were ill with severe sinusitis and ear infection. The father was up front in mentioning that they had no money to pay, but would appreciate the medical help. I decided not only to take care of the immediate problem, but arranged to get a job for the father, who was a country singer. His wife was full term pregnant. My Ob-Gyn friend who used to use my office for consultation agreed to deliver the baby for a small fee. When I decided to move to Atlanta and be semi-retired, he came into my office and handed me an envelope full of cash for my services. When I resisted, he said that his father had sold his old home along with the surrounding land for millions of dollars and sent him a large portion of it. During the early years of 1980s, AAPI was founded. I am proud to say that I was one of the founders along with my close friends Drs. Shah and Chandragiri. We have been blessed with two beautiful children who studied hard and have become self sufficient in their lives. Since our move to Georgia, my wife who is a retired Psychiatrist encouraged me to stay busy. She has been a great influence and supporter of all my deeds. Initially I started a free clinic for the uninsured at a Suwanee Church. Since this church was located in a wealthy area, Dr. Sreeni Gangasani (whom I befriended soon after my arrival in Atlanta) and I decided to open a clinic under the umbrella of GAPI. The sense of fulfillment, satisfaction and happiness in doing this type of volunteer work is priceless. I feel glad that it helps others who are in real need. I also volunteer at Clarkston clinic where the majority are immigrants from various countries. My mother, who is a huge influence in my life, says that we are born empty-handed and will die empty-handed. It is not going to the temples or buying expensive cars, homes or jewels that will earn peace in our lives. It is helping poor souls, the creations of God whose blessings will go with us. My grandfather was one of the wealthiest men in town. But today, he is remembered for the girls’ school he built in Tiruppur, Tamil Nadu, which has led to educating thousands of young females. This is the world’s largest all female school to date. Despite the fact my wife is a three times breast cancer survivor and my recent diagnosis of bone cancer, I feel blessed to continue the work I have chosen to do. I forget I have cancer. I strongly believe in the power of prayer. If I do good for others without expecting anything in return, maybe they will bless me. This is what keeps me going. My motto is what Mahatma Gandhi taught us: you must be the change you want to see in this world. I am in no competition with anyone. I sleep with the satisfaction that I managed to help someone today.” – Dr Jay Sampat Dr. Jay N. Sampat, MD is an internist who practices wholistic medicine and heads the GAPI free clinic located in the Global Mall. Admission & Open to the Public We want to say Thank you to the Chickasaw Nation for supporting us from the beginning to have our Celebration at Lone Star Park. They saw the need for advocacy, education and cultural preservation for American Indians. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . The “American Indian Heritage Day in Texas” is celebrating its 5th Anniversary of the State Observance Law in Texas on September 30, 2017 at the Lone Star Park in Grand Prairie, Texas. We are focusing on the rich culture and preservation of the Five Civilized Tribes. We are very excited to work with the Choctaw Nation of Oklahoma in making this year’s theme “Echoes of (Tru).th” a success. This celebration gives us an opportunity to share our knowledge. With Choctaw Nation alliance, we can bring a new forward thinking to educate a new audience, a new generation and non-Indians . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . + Friday, September 29, 2017 + Educational Observance Day in the State of Texas is a day for public schools, organizations, and businesses. AIHD will serve as an on-line resource. We are in the building stages to bring American Indians to the table, to become more of a focus as Indigenous People before the United States. We are also in steps to expand our voice beyond the Dallas/Fort Worth community to the State of Texas. This is an exciting adventure! . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . + Saturday, September 30, 2017 + AIHD is hosting the 5th Annual Celebration “Echoes of (Tru).th”. The morning presentation will highlight a very challenging topic, “American Indian Stickball - The creation of the game and the forerunner to Lacrosse”. We are introducing the Southeastern Style of the Game. The noon presentation will be Tribal Connection whereby recognition of tribal dignitaries, Strikingsticks Award and the honoring of the Elder of the Year. The afternoon presentation will be a discussion on “The Five Civilized Tribes - Sacredness of Tribal Songs and Social Dances”. The songs will focus on the church hymns sung with tribal dialect, stomp dance songs and social dance songs.
